        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.8b13160"><meta property="og:title" content="Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/acsami.8b13160.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries are regarded as one of the most promising energy storage systems, but the choice of anode material is still facing great challenges. Biomass carbon materials were explored for their low cost and wide range of sources. Here, a hard carbon material with a “honeycomb” structure using pine pollen (PP) as a precursor was successfully prepared and applied as an anode. The initial discharge capacity can reach 370 mA h g–1 at a current density of 0.1 A g–1. After cycling 200 times, the reversible capacity also stabled at 203.3 mA h g–1 with the retention rate of 98%. We further studied the sodium storage mechanism by different methods, especially the Na+ diffusivity coefficient (DNa+) calculated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique, which was more accurate. Interestingly, the trend of DNa+ coincides with cyclic voltammetry curves. Carbonized PP exhibited excellent electrochemical properties because of its three-dimensional structure and larger layer spacing (∼0.41 nm), which reduces the resistance of sodium ions to intercalation and deintercalation."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/acsami.8b13160.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318891cef7f54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318891cef7f54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '190a6672-827a-441e-b788-23c3e86dc3f7'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

charset="utf-8" async="" src="https://script.hotjar.com/modules.f98993dba31a932bc912.js"></script><script id="yui__dyn_0" type="text/javascript" src="https://www2.snapengage.com/chatjs/ServiceGetConfig?w=921a4ec7-cecf-43a6-afa5-6cd1346520b1"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/lazy-imports-6922fe6a4ca6b2e0cd9a.js"></script><script type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318891cef7f54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="9318891cef7f54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Honeycomb-like Hard Carbon Derived from Pine Pollen as High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13160", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13160&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries are regarded as one of the most promising energy storage systems, but the choice of anode material is still facing great challenges. Biomass carbon materials were explored for their low cost and wide range of sources. Here, a hard carbon material with a “honeycomb” structure using pine pollen (PP) as a precursor was successfully prepared and applied as an anode. The initial discharge capacity can reach 370 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. After cycling 200 times, the reversible capacity also stabled at 203.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with the retention rate of 98%. We further studied the sodium storage mechanism by different methods, especially the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) calculated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique, which was more accurate. Interestingly, the trend of <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> coincides with cyclic voltammetry curves. Carbonized PP exhibited excellent electrochemical properties because of its three-dimensional structure and larger layer spacing (∼0.41 nm), which reduces the resistance of sodium ions to intercalation and deintercalation.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D42796%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYanjia%2BZhang%252C%2BXue%2BLi%252C%2BPeng%2BDong%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D49%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b13160%26title%3DHoneycomb-like%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BDerived%2Bfrom%2BPine%2BPollen%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D42803%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Energy+Storage&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_energy%20storage">energy storage</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Biomass-derived&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_biomass-derived">biomass-derived</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon">hard carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As the environmental crisis aggravated, the development of clean and recyclable systems of energy storage has become more urgent. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 1 to 6">(1−6)</a> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are widely utilized in energy storage systems, such as hydraulics, firepower, wind power, and solar power plants, because of their good cycle performance and high energy density. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> However, with the increase of market demand, lithium metal has been insufficient for a long time, which leads to higher and higher prices, directly increasing the production cost of various lithium-based batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> In the long run, there is an urgent need to find a new storage system that rivals lithium-based batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> The sodium element is considered the most promising alternative to lithium based on their similar physicochemical properties, and the most attractive reason is that sodium is widely distributed and inexpensive. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 12 to 16">(12−16)</a> The charge and discharge behavior of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is similar to that of LIBs, but the diameter of sodium ions is 0.204 nm, which is longer than lithium ions (0.152 nm). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> The larger volume leads to a slow process of kinetics on sodium ions’ insertion–extraction in the electrode material, causing a lower reversible capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> The key factor to solve this problem is to design an electrode material with larger layer spacing to store sodium ions.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among a wide variety of electrode materials, carbon materials that benefit from low cost, high conductivity, stable physical and chemical properties are most widely used. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21−23)</a> Structurally, the carbon materials used to store sodium fall into two broad categories: ordered soft carbon <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and disordered hard carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> Soft carbon is so tightly packed that it gives sodium ions less access to the interior of the material, whereas hard carbon provides more channels. Biomass-derived carbon is mostly hard carbon with the widest variety of sources, diverse structures and topographies, as well as its own multiple elements that do not require postprocessing. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 26 to 30">(26−30)</a> The use of biomass-derived carbon in the negative electrode of SIBs has made some progress. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> For example, Wang <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> made peanut skin as a precursor, prepared hierarchical porous carbon with layer spacing of 0.37–0.39 nm; the first discharge capacity can be up to 1275 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, but the Coulomb efficiency is only 29%. Cao <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> successfully processed rapeseed seeds into carbon material, and the distance between layers was 0.39 nm, at 25 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; the battery showed a discharge capacity of 237 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for the first cycle. Zhu <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> carbonized cherry into a honeycomb-like fold of carbon material in a high-temperature pyrolysis method; the layer spacing can enlarge up to 0.44 nm, and the capacity retention after 100 cycles is still maintained at 99.3% at the current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In addition, the hard carbon derived from banana peels, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> apple biowaste, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> and nature leaves <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> has also been studied as anode material.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this paper, we successfully derived hard carbon material with “honeycomb” morphology using pine pollen (PP) as a precursor, with a specific surface area of 171.54 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and a larger layer spacing of 0.41 nm. When carbonized PP (CPP) was applied as anode material of SIBs, the discharge capacity was still maintained at 203.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles, and CPP also exhibited outstanding rate performance at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> with reversible capacity of 87 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Besides the structural advantages, we carried out a detailed study of the sodium storage mechanism in CPP. First, the sodium storage mechanism was analyzed by cyclic voltammetry (CV). Studied by CV under different scan rates and <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>, all the results prove that the reasoning is correct. What is more, the method by which we calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> is more accurate, and interestingly, the trend of sodium diffusion coefficient coincides with the CV curve. In conclusion, all the analyses of the sodium storage mechanism show that CPP is an excellent electrode material, and we believe that the outstanding performance of CPP can promote the application of biomass in SIBs. The synthesis process is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, which contains the microstructure and application of CPP.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Preparation scheme of CPP.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13160&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Synthesis of CPP</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The preparation process of the CPP is described as follows: PP was carbonized at 900 °C for 1 h in argon atmosphere; the temperature increased at the rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. The obtained material was dissolved in 0.5 M sulfuric acid solution to remove the organic impurities. Finally, the product was washed to a neutral pH value, and dried overnight.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of CPP was examined through scanning electron microscopy (TESCAN VEGA3, SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (Tecnai G2 F20 S-Twin, TEM). The composition and chemical state were measured by X-ray diffraction (Rigaku MiniFlex600 powder diffractometer, XRD), Raman spectroscopy (LabRAM HR Evolution), and PHI5000 Versaprobe-II spectrometer type X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller method was used to calculate the surface area, and CPP was first outgassed for 4 h at 150 °C under vacuum, then investigated on an automatic sorption analyzer (ASAP 2460) at 77 K according to the nitrogen adsorption–desorption process of pressure changes.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The sodium-ion battery was assembled by using 2016-type coin cells. The anode electrode was prepared as follows. We mixed the CPP, Super P, and polyvinylidenefluoride in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone with a mass ratio of 8:1:1 to form a slurry. It was then spread onto a copper foil with the slurry thickness of 100 nm, and dried overnight at 120 °C. In order to make the material and copper foil bond better and closer, we polished the pole piece. Sodium metal was used as the cathode, separate cathode and anode with glass fiber (Whatman), and the electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture solvent of ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (1:1). All the steps of battery assembly were carried out in an argon-protected glove box. Both the moisture and oxygen contents were under 0.1 ppm. At room temperature, we performed the charge–discharge performance on the testing system (CT2001A, LAND, China).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The structure of CPP was studied by XRD patterns and Raman spectra. As <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> demonstrates, two characteristic peaks of CPP are situated at 2θ ≈ 24° and 43°, which correspond to the (002) and (100) planes of carbon, showing that we successfully carbonized the CPP into a representative highly disordered hard carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The value of <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> was calculated as 1.51 nm, which represented the layer thickness that along in the direction of <i>c</i>-axis between graphite microcrystalline. In addition, the empirical parameter <i>R</i> (2.03) suggests that our CPP contains just a few pieces of graphite sheets. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> According to the literature, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> the graphitization degree of CPP can reflect the capacity in storage sodium ions. On the basis of the analysis, we conducted a Raman test to further test the disorder degree. As <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> shows, the spectra contain two prominent wide peaks at about 1343 (D band) and 1580 (G band) cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The peak ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is 1.07, indicating that the CPP has an amorphous structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of CPP are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>, which belongs to the IV type isotherm, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> inferring that the CPP was a mesoporous structure; this may be due to the gas generated during the carbonization process. On the basis of the classification of IUPAC, the relative pressure (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub>) ranging from 0.4 to 1.0 shows a typical H3-style hysteresis loop, indicating that the pores of CPP are disordered, which fits well with the TEM results. The distribution of pore size was acquired by using the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) model, and the data were based on the adsorption branch to prevent the spurious peaks. Through the analysis of BET, the specific surface area of CPP is 171.54 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. Simultaneously, the pore size of average is calculated as 4.82 nm. What is more, it can be seen from the inset figure in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a> that most of the pores are spread at 2–4 nm, which is a favorable condition for storing sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The morphological characterization of CPP is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. It is obvious that CPP shows a honeycomb-like structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a), and the surface of CPP has lots of irregular pores (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), which is beneficial to store sodium ions. It can be observed from the high-resolution TEM graphic (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c) that CPP illustrates a representative hard carbon material construction, which contains unordered graphite microcrystals in a short distance, which contributes to the higher reversible specific capacity. We further confirmed the highly disordered microstructures by selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c) with electron diffraction rings, and there were no diffraction spots on the dispersed diffraction rings, proving that CPP has the characteristics of hard carbon materials. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, compared with graphite (0.335 nm), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> the layer spacing of CPP (0.41 nm) is much bigger. The larger layer spacing promotes the transmission and storage of sodium ions, also improving the cycling stability. These results are supported by the XRD and Raman data.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM (a) and TEM (b,c) images of PP with the SAED image in (c); (d) layer spacing of CPP along the direction of the yellow line segment.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13160&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The XPS survey spectrum is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. From the full spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a), we can find that CPP primarily consists of C and O. In order to confirm the chemical condition of these two main elements, the high resolutions of C 1s and O 1s were fitted. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b displays three major characteristic peaks at 284.6, 285.4, and 287.2 eV, corresponding to the C–C, C–O, and C═O bonds, respectively. The C–C bond that basically exists in the nondefective graphite lattice has the highest peak area ratio of 57 at. %; then the C–O (26 at. %) and C═O (17 at. %) bonds lie in the defective graphite lattice. The characteristic peaks of the O 1s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) located in 530.9 and 532.4 eV correspond to the C═O and C–OH bonds, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> According to the literature, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref43" aria-label="reference 35 and 43">(35,43)</a> these functional groups that contain oxygen may contribute to the increased capacity because of the increased active site of the redox reaction on the CPP surface. In addition, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> demonstrates the specific elements’ percentage. It can be observed that our CPP also contains a small amount of nitrogen, which can promote the storage of sodium ions.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XPS survey spectra of CPP specimens; high-resolution XPS C 1s spectra (b) and O 1s (c).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13160&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">CV tests were conducted to investigate the storage process of sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the first three laps taking 0.01–3 V as the electrochemical window at a sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. There are three reduction peaks during the first discharge process; the obvious one near 0.95 V is mainly on account of the reaction between other functional groups and sodium ions, whereas the slight one near 0.13 V is likely due to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer. Notably, two reduction peaks vanished in the second and third cycles, demonstrating that the reactions that happened in the first cycle were irreversible, and well explained the low initial Coulombic efficiency of CPP. In addition, there is a pair of sharp redox peaks near 0.01 V, which indicates the insertion–extraction process of sodium ions in the mezzanine of graphitic microcrystallites. It can be seen that there is a large slope from 1 to 0.01 V, illustrating the adsorption process of sodium ions on the CPP surface, until the voltage turns to 0.01 V, the surface tends to be saturated, sodium ions primarily in the form of embedded, showing a sharp oxidation peak at low potential. All of these correspond to the oxidation process.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of CPP: (a) CV curves; (b) galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling profiles; (c) cycling performance at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; rate performance (d) and rate performance comparison of PP with the state-of-the-art in the literature (e).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13160&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The discharge–charge profiles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The initial discharge and charge capacity can reach 370.1 and 221.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. However, the Coulombic efficiency is just 59.8%; this is basically due to the irreversible reactions on the surface of the material, which fits well with the CV results. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c studies the cyclability and Coulombic efficiency of CPP. At 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the CPP anode obtains a reversible capacity of 207.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. After 200 cycles, it still remained at 203.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with the retention rate of 98%, demonstrating high cycling stability. At the same time, we find that the discharge curve is mostly divided into two parts, one is the slope region and the other is the platform region, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> which are located in the voltage of 1.2–0.1 and 0.1–0 V, respectively. Combined with the CV results, the slope section represents the adsorption process of sodium ions; the platform section refers to the insertion–extraction process. In order to ensure the superiority of the CPP electrode, we further tested the rate performance of CPP. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, the CPP electrode presents average reversible capacities of 236.3, 192.8, 168.5, 140.3, 118.4, 87.3, and 201.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current rates of 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, 5000, and 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. We are surprised that when it returns to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity can reach 201.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which is 85.2% of the initial value, indicating the stable structure and high reversible capacity during cycles. This phenomenon mainly benefits from the expanded graphite microcrystalline layer spacing, which is conducive to the transmission of sodium ions. We have also made some comparison about the rate performance among other biomass carbons in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e, CPP still outstanding at high current density. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref35 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 34, 35 and 46 to 49">(34,35,46−49)</a> More details can be seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> displays the Nyquist impedance plots of the sodium-ion battery with the CPP electrode for different cycles; the test was under the condition of 10<sup>5</sup> Hz to 10<sup>–2</sup> Hz and scanning rates of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. All curves consist of a semicircular arc in the medium-high frequency section and a linear tail in the low section. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, the curves are fitted by the corresponding equivalent circuit, where <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> means the Ohmic contact resistance, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> correspond to charge transfer resistance, CPE means double-layer capacitance, and <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> refers to the Warburg impedance of sodium ion diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a> Notably, <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> appearing after the first cycle represents the diffusion resistance caused by the SEI film. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a> As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>, all curves are fitted with the error rate under 5%. It is obvious that both <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> + <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> increase as the cycle progresses, but compared with <i>R</i><sub>s</sub>, the increment of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> + <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> decreases gradually, showing that the SEI film was obtained primarily in the first few cycles, which is consistent with the CV results.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, CV under several scan rates (0.1–1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) is a predominant way to further study the reaction mechanism. The relationship is as follows according to the formula <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curves for the CPP electrode between 0.1 and 3.0 V at different scan rates; (b) log(<i>ip</i>) vs log(<i>v</i>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13160&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In this formula, <i>i</i> corresponds to the peak current, <i>a</i> and exponent <i>b</i> are adjustable values. Notably, <i>b</i> has two representative values. One value of <i>b</i> tends to be 0.5, indicating that semi-infinite diffusion controlled the current, whereas the other tends to be 1, demonstrating that the current is surface-controlled. From the CV curves, we can observe that the amount of sodium stored in the CPP is inversely proportional to the scan rates. The reason is that a high scan rate almost gives no time for sodium ions to diffuse. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> We also find that as the scanning rate decreases, the potential difference (Δ<i>E</i> = |<i>E</i><sub>O</sub> – <i>E</i><sub>R</sub>|) between the oxidation peak (<i>E</i><sub>O</sub>) and the reduction peak (<i>E</i><sub>R</sub>) gradually decreases, indicating that the CPP electrode is a reversible reaction, and can directly enhance the cycle ability of the sodium-ion battery. In order to obtain the value of <i>b</i> more intuitively, we converted the above formula, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b; the sweep rate satisfies the linear regression (<i>R</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.9996), the <i>b</i> value 0.4799 represents the slope in the converted formula, very close to 0.5, as mentioned above, showing that semi-infinite diffusion mainly controls the current. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref41 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref41 ref51" aria-label="reference 33, 41 and 51">(33,41,51)</a> That fits well with the charge–discharge profiles, and gives a good explanation about the capacity obtained in the low-pressure area.</div><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the excellent capability of CPP, the diffusivity coefficient of sodium ions (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) was tested by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) with a 0.2 C pulse current for 30 min between the rest gap for 1 h. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57 ref58 ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56 ref57 ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 56 to 59">(56−59)</a> According to Fick’s second law (reversal of reaction <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>), the calculation formula is as follows<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the weight of CPP on the pole piece, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> represents the molar volume of carbon, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> represents the molecular mass of carbon, <i>S</i> represents the area of the active material on a single pole piece, τ represents the pulse time, Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> represents the voltage difference at the time when the voltage reaches the steady state in a single GITT process, and <i>L</i> represents the thickness of the pole piece. In order to simplify the formula, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b shows a single GITT diagram, further comparing the relationship between voltage and τ<sup>1/2</sup>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c, they satisfy the linear relationship. Therefore, the above formula can be simplified (reversal of reaction <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>) as<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13160u_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) GITT curve of CPP as electrode material; (b) single GITT titration curve; (c) linear fitting of discharge voltages <i>E</i> vs τ<sup>1/2</sup> in a single GITT titration curve; (d) relationship between voltage and log <i>D</i>Li<sub><sup>+</sup></sub> during the discharge and charge process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/asset/images/large/am-2018-13160u_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13160&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d, the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> was expressed in logarithmic form. With the depth of discharge, sodium ions that adsorbed on the surface of the electrode material of sodium ions gradually become saturated. Some of the sodium ions enter into the material through diffusion. However, because of the influence of external resistance, the kinetic process is slow, so the diffusion coefficient of the low-pressure region drops sharply. In the same way, a large number of sodium ions leave the surface at the beginning of the charge process, and the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> increased rapidly. With the reaction proceeding, the sodium ions on the surface became progressively lesser, so the sodium ions embedded in the intergranular graphite crystallites began to detach and the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> decreased. What is exciting is that the trend of <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> in this paper coincides with the CV curve. This undoubtedly reaffirms our inference on the sodium storage mechanism of CPP. We attribute this to the more accurate calculation of the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>, different from the calculation method of previous reports; the parameter <i>S</i> here is the surface area of all the active materials on a single pole piece, not the contact area on the pole piece. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58 ref60" aria-label="reference 58 and 60">(58,60)</a></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this paper, the PP was used as precursor, and a hard carbon material with a honeycomb structure was successfully prepared. The application of CPP as the negative electrode showed excellent electrochemical performance. With the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity of discharge could reach 370.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>; the initial Coulombic efficiency was 59.8%. CPP also exhibited superior cycling stability; the reversible capacity stabled at 203 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles and even had a high capacity of 87.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when it turned to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, demonstrating better rate performance than other biomass carbon materials. On the basis of various electrochemical test data, it was concluded that the storage of sodium ion was mainly divided into two stages. The first stage was the high-voltage slope in the beginning of discharge; sodium ions during this period were basically adsorbed on the surface as the predominant form. When the sodium ion on the surface of the material tends to be saturated, sodium ions in the low voltage platform area mainly through the embedded form. In order to confirm this theory, we also tested the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>; the result was completely consistent with the inference. All of these excellent properties were inseparable from the material itself; the three-dimensional microstructure provides space for the reaction, a larger surface area provided an area for surface adsorption, and mesopores also promote the sodium ion. The most important thing was that the large interlayer spacing reduces the resistance of sodium ions to intercalation and deintercalation. In summary, it provided the ideas for other types of energy storage systems because of the low production cost and simple preparation process.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b13160" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b13160</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD pattern of CPP; Raman pattern of CPP; nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and BJH pore size distributions of CPP, the inset figure is a partial enlargement of the pore size distribution of 2–14 nm; elemental ratio on the surface of CPP; comparison of the rate performance of different biomass-derived carbon materials; electrochemical impedance spectra of the CPP anodes at different cycles and equivalent circuits; and impedance parameters of CPP electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13160/suppl_file/am8b13160_si_001.pdf">am8b13160_si_001.pdf (271.41 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b13160" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
